  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_32b89ffa-a44a-4704-bbab-a45cd306768d'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4333983192514517716">This engaging series of childhood recollections tells about an ideal school in Tokyo during World War II that combined learning with fun, freedom, and love. This unusual school had old railroad cars for classrooms, and it was run by an extraordinary man--its founder and headmaster, Sosaku Kobayashi--who was a firm believer in freedom of expression and activity.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11403708442356110587"><b>Madogiwa no Totto-chan = Totto-chan: The Little Girl at the Window, Tetsuko Kuroyanagi </b><br/><br/>Totto-chan, the Little Girl at the Window is a children's book written by Japanese television personality and UNICEF Goodwill Ambassador Tetsuko Kuroyanagi. The book was published originally as "Madogiwa no Totto-chan" in 1981, and became an instant bestseller in Japan. <br/><br/>The book is about the values of the unconventional education that Kuroyanagi received at Tomoe Gakuen, a Tokyo elementary school founded by ed</span>
  <span id="freeText11403708442356110587" style="display:none"><b>Madogiwa no Totto-chan = Totto-chan: The Little Girl at the Window, Tetsuko Kuroyanagi </b><br /><br />Totto-chan, the Little Girl at the Window is a children's book written by Japanese television personality and UNICEF Goodwill Ambassador Tetsuko Kuroyanagi. The book was published originally as "Madogiwa no Totto-chan" in 1981, and became an instant bestseller in Japan. <br /><br />The book is about the values of the unconventional education that Kuroyanagi received at Tomoe Gakuen, a Tokyo elementary school founded by educator Sosaku Kobayashi during World War II, and it is considered her childhood memoir.<br /><br /> The book begins with Totto-chan's mother coming to know of her daughter's expulsion from public school. Her mother realizes that what Totto-chan needs is a school where more freedom of expression is permitted. Thus, she takes Totto-chan to meet the headmaster of the new school, Mr. Kobayashi. <div id="review_653900268" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/653900268" />
    <a title="Mizuki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16842102-mizuki"><img alt="Mizuki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368277474p2/16842102.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/653900268?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mizuki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mizuki" href="/user/show/16842102-mizuki">Mizuki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16842102-mizuki?shelf=chinese-translation">chinese-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16842102-mizuki?shelf=japanese-authors">japanese-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer653900268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer939344970030994610"><i>Totto-chan: The Little Girl at the Window</i> was written by Tetsuko Kuroyanagi, a Japanese actress, who wrote this memoir based on her childhood experience at Tomoe Gakuen primary school, before World War II.<br/><br/>Totto-chan, who was labeled as a troublemaker by her teacher, was forced to leave school. Her anxious mother then took her to Tomoe Gakuen, a school ran by headmaster Mr. Kobayashi, who had utterly different views on schooling than the other educators of his time. He was an educator who believe</span>
  <span id="freeText939344970030994610" style="display:none"><i>Totto-chan: The Little Girl at the Window</i> was written by Tetsuko Kuroyanagi, a Japanese actress, who wrote this memoir based on her childhood experience at Tomoe Gakuen primary school, before World War II.<br /><br />Totto-chan, who was labeled as a troublemaker by her teacher, was forced to leave school. Her anxious mother then took her to Tomoe Gakuen, a school ran by headmaster Mr. Kobayashi, who had utterly different views on schooling than the other educators of his time. He was an educator who believed respect, encouragement and freedom are keys to a child's better development than punishment and boxing the child according to adults' own liking.<br /><br />I first read this book when I was a kid, probably about Totto-chan's age or a bit older. Reading this book/memoir as a kid is the same like reading a fairytale of a little girl stumbling into Wonderland. In a sense, Totto-chan's experience in Tomoe Gakuen really is nothing less than a fairytale comes true: Totto-chan and her friends studied in classrooms made of train-carriages (due to the lack of fund for real classrooms), their teachers taught them to dance, to express themselves freely and gave out nontraditional lessons, their headmaster spent time listening to what the children had to say and talked to them like equals...there're so much more to be explored as we follow Totto-chan making new discoveries about herself and the world around her. And through the writing, you can sense there's always love, so much love surrounding the children: love among parents and children, among Totto-chan and friends, among teachers and students.<br /><br />But happiness always comes to an end, just like every childhood must. With the approaching of war and food shortage, Totto-chan slowly learnt the harsh truth of life through the death of her friends, etc. The small wonders and adventures of the children was <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[slowly and surely engulfed by war, eventually Tomoe Gakuen was burnt to the ground during a bombing, and was never rebuilt.<br /><br />This is a heartbreaking end to a wonderful story.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I admit I had tear in my eyes whenever I recalled the events and the endearing characters in <i>Totto-chan</i>. It is a book which is both insightful, rich and moving at the same time. If you ever encountered someone who's like headmaster Mr. Kobayashi, then count yourself a lucky soul.</span>
  <a data-text-id="939344970030994610" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3474956152709450053">I believe books can do magic. They can hypnotize you, they can make you laugh, they can even break your heart or they can take you to a journey you may have never dreamt of! Yeah, books are magic! <br/><br/>And this book of Totto chan is the greatest magic I have come across recently.   <span id="freeText3474956152709450053" style="display:none">I believe books can do magic. They can hypnotize you, they can make you laugh, they can even break your heart or they can take you to a journey you may have never dreamt of! Yeah, books are magic! <br /><br />And this book of Totto chan is the greatest magic I have come across recently. I donâ€™t think only five stars can truly express my feelings for this book!<br /><br />Totto Chan, a first grader, Japanese girl lived in a world of her own. She loved to talk, loved to make new friends and always has a heap of questions in her little head about everything! Sometimes she wanted to be a spy and sometimes a stationmaster! Precious little girl Totto chan! But this attitude of her got her expelled from her boring, traditional school! Can you believe it? They actually expelled a first grader!<br /><br />Young Totto chan had no idea what might have happened while her mother took her to a new school, Tomoe. It was not a bit like the previous one. In that school, the classes were taken in abandoned railroad cars! Yes, real railroad cars! This made Totto chan to fall in love with her new school at the first sight! <br /><br />When she met the headmaster, Mr. Kobayashi, the first question she asked him was if he was a headmaster or a stationmaster! The headmaster laughed and listened everything Totto Chan had to say without yawning. That made her the happiest person of the world! She couldnâ€™t wait any longer to start her first day at that fun school!<br /><br />Totto chans days were like dreams in there. The school didnâ€™t force them to study a particular subject. They could start their day with any subjects they liked. They would sing before eating----<br /><br /><p><i><b> "Chew, chew, chew it well,<br /><br />Everything you eat;<br /><br />Chew it and chew it and chew it and chew it,<br /><br />Your rice and fish and meat!" </b></i><br /><br />Fascinating, right? <br /><br />The teachers would also take them on a stroll after the lunch. They even used their assembly hall as camping site! <br /><br />But things went bad during the Second World War. People died, got wounded, cities got bombed, there were scarcity of food! <br /><br />Totto chans happy days also turned into gloomy ones! <br />At first, she lost her best friend Yasuaki chan died. I was literally crying when Totto chan whispered to Yasuaki-chan <br /><br /></p><p>
  <i>
    <b>"Maybe we'll meet again somewhere when we're much older. And maybe your polio will be cured by then."</b>
  </i>
</p><br /><br />Then she lost her another best friend, her dog Rocky. She was sad, really sad.<br /><br />But the saddest thing happened laterâ€¦.her school; her beloved school was burned down! The dream of Mr. Kobayashi disappeared amid of flamesâ€¦.. <br /><br />These are not fantasies! These things actually happened! The little Totto chan herself wrote this book. Yes, it is the story of Japanese celebrity author Tetsuko Kuroyanagi. <br /><br />At first I thought itâ€™s a fiction. But it turned out to be nonfiction! I love this book so much! Totto chan was really lucky and so were the other kids who had the opportunity to spend their childhood in a school like Tomoe, which taught them to live and laugh. <br /><br />This book has filled my heart with some innumerable emotions. I laughed, I cried, I felt happy, I started missing my school life! Although it wasnâ€™t as dreamy as Tomoe, I loved it and I still miss it. School life was undoubtedly the best part of my life just like Totto chans! :)</span>

<div id="review_5464148" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5464148" />
    <a title="Hantz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/328718-hantz"><img alt="Hantz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1217544546p2/328718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5464148?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hantz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hantz" href="/user/show/328718-hantz">Hantz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">my sister,my friends</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer5464148&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5464148" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13517277678076430222">Gosh at last i finished this book, took me 2 days to finish. And here is my review<br/><br/>Splendid and awesome, highly recommended to those who are interested in childhood story and education.<br/>Totto chan is a girl that full of passions and very active. With such personal traits it is not easy for a traditional school to educate her. She ended up being expelled from her first school because the teacher just couldnâ€™t manage her, she is such a nuisance to the others. Teachers eventually gave up on her and s</span>
  <span id="freeText13517277678076430222" style="display:none">Gosh at last i finished this book, took me 2 days to finish. And here is my review<br /><br />Splendid and awesome, highly recommended to those who are interested in childhood story and education.<br />Totto chan is a girl that full of passions and very active. With such personal traits it is not easy for a traditional school to educate her. She ended up being expelled from her first school because the teacher just couldnâ€™t manage her, she is such a nuisance to the others. Teachers eventually gave up on her and sadly she was expelled from her first school.<br /><br />Her mother then sent her to Tomoe, and alternative school where the studying activities done in fun way, the pupils were given so much room to express their creativity, they were encouraged to go out and play, understand the nature, get the first hand experience from the real life practitioner (the farmer). There is no such thing as fixed seating arrangement in this school, children were free to chose in which chair they wanted to seat, and even they can even pick the subject they want to study, in short they were given such luxury of freedom which they wont get it in a conventional school, thanks to head master Mr Kobayashi the person she adored very much. He is children loving - teacher, who traveled and study the education in Europe and came back to Japan to set up the school of his dream.<br /><br />Tobby Chan enjoyed school life very much, and this book is compilation of the experiences she went through during that school period, the setting was in Japanese world war 2 period.<br /><br />There are some story that touched me and remind me how innocent we were as a children. When she found coin in the train and felt guilty but somehow she hid the money in the thicket hole only eventually found out later that the money mysteriously disappeared (I still thinking where the hell the money gone). The other time when she jumped onto the piece of paper that laying on the school ground only to find out that it was a temporary cover of a cesspool, and other time she ended up jumping into mixture of wall plaster and get suck in there until mother come to the rescue.<br /><br />And as a child she had to deal with the feeling of loss. She lost her German shepherd (Rocky, the big one who is very loyal to her yet he once bitten her ear to bleed), lost his buddy Takahashi with whom she climbed her school tree together (this is very sad part). She went to hospital to visit the wounded soldiers, and console them by singing some children songs and yet since she didnâ€™t know the songs she only stood there silence and she eventually sang her heart out the very simple song the used to sing before having their lunch and miraculously brought the soldier to tears (very touching part)<br /><br />The book is ended with American bombardment, the school was destroyed completely, and until Mr Kobayahi died he couldnâ€™t rebuild the school and it turned out that the very area where the school stood (it consisted of some used car trains) now converted into a shopping mall. Sad ending indeed. But all in all it is very good book to read and worth reading, highly recommended one !!!!<br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

  <span id="freeText14417221654535693279" style="display:none">I stumbled upon an important book the other day.<br /><br />I probably found it because K is next to J and when Iâ€™m in a used bookstore, I always look for Tove Jansson books.<br /><br />On this day at Retold Tales I noticed a little book called Totto-Chan: The Little Girl at the Window by Tetsuko Kuroyanagi. The brief dust jacket description told me it was a personâ€™s true recollections about a school of â€œfun, freedom, and loveâ€ that met in old train cars.<br /><br />I started reading it there on the floor and knew Iâ€™d buy it. I finished it in just a couple days. Tetsuko Kuroyanagi writes her memories of this unusual and special school in Japan during the time of World War II. Itâ€™s a beautiful and inspiring little book.<br /><br />The school, Tomoe, was created by someone named Sosaku Kobayashi who believed children should learn in freedom with much self-expression. He told his teachers â€œLeave them to nature. Donâ€™t cramp their ambitions. Their dreams are bigger than yours.â€<br /><br />The author writes many beautiful stories of the small and large ways Sosaku Kobayashi crafted a loving space for children of different abilities, interests, and backgrounds. He and his wife made sure each child had enough food to eat, could find and pursue what intrigued them, and felt valued and good. Tetsuko writes about Sosakuâ€™s habit of telling her many, many times â€œYouâ€™re really a good girl, you know.â€ and how she kept that message in her heart throughout life.<br /><br />She first came to Tomoe after being expelled from First Grade at another school. At that school, she endlessly opened and closed her desk, ran to the window during class, and other things her teacher just couldnâ€™t tolerate. At Tomoe, her life changed.<br /><br />The school was not fancy. Instead, it was full of enthusiasm, creativity, and trust that children will follow their interests and learn well if given the right support and freedom. As John Holt much later wrote â€œAll I am sayingâ€¦ can be summed up in two words: Trust Children.â€<br /><br />Totto-chan is a beautiful book showing one model of a school that doesnâ€™t require students to conform, but instead nurtures and celebrates them.<br /><br />I think you should know going in that things end very sadly. No one is injured in the event, but Tomoe is destroyed by an American bomber and the children must move on to other schools. I appreciated knowing this before reaching that point in the story. Without time to prepare myself, I think it would have been too devastating. The book ends with more information on what each person went on to do in life.<br /><br />Totto-chan is a book I missed until now and Iâ€™m so glad I stumbled upon it. (Arenâ€™t quirky bookstores great?) Letâ€™s take the inspiration of Tomoe and create more and more opportunities for children to grown up in nature, in love, and with the reminder, over and over, that they are good.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5551468270379583842">Such a charming book!<br/> <br/>I do have to say, I would have finished this much sooner...it is normally a nice, quick read...but life with my 3 kids has been busy lately, so it took longer than normal.<br/><br/>Totto-Chan was very fortunate to be able to go to school at Tomoe.<br/>I wish there were more schools like it.<br/>I love that the headmaster really was committed to these children and their education.   <span id="freeText5551468270379583842" style="display:none">Such a charming book!<br /> <br />I do have to say, I would have finished this much sooner...it is normally a nice, quick read...but life with my 3 kids has been busy lately, so it took longer than normal.<br /><br />Totto-Chan was very fortunate to be able to go to school at Tomoe.<br />I wish there were more schools like it.<br />I love that the headmaster really was committed to these children and their education. Making sure to let Totto-Chan know..."...you really are a good girl, you know." Always giving her that positive reinforcement, which stayed with her throughout her life. What an impact he had on these children.<br /><br />I really enjoyed every chapter...but a few phrases stood out, more than others. In "Shabby Old School"...when the children linked arms and chanted the song that Totto-Chan made up, defending her school...."It was their hearts that were unison, more than their voices..."<br />They truly had a special bond at Tomoe.<br /><br />The author wrote that she would be content if she had made people realize how even a little girl, like Totto-Chan, given the right kind of adult influence, can become a person who is able to get along with others.<br /><br />I wish we had more educators like Mr. Kobayashi in this world.<br />What a wonderful tribute to him, this book is.<br /><br />There is so much more I could write about why I loved this book, and other little details to point out, but why try to re-write the whole book. It was a wonderful treat!<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5551468270379583842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1008004060" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1008004060" />
    <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all"><img alt="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406361683p2/20738920.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1008004060?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all">Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=japanese-authors">japanese-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=couldn-t-put-it-down">couldn-t-put-it-down</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=life-affirming-cosy">life-affirming-cosy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=thinking-people-s-books">thinking-people-s-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=memoirs">memoirs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1008004060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1753129113652729177">I usually avoid "wonderful teacher" books like the plague, but then those books are often written by the teacher him/herself, or ghost-written with their approval, and can be oh so self-congratulatory. This one, however, was written by a student whose school experiences truly formed her character.<br/><br/>Totto-chan is expelled from school (in first grade!) for being disruptive. She's not hostile or violent, just curious and easily bored. I suppose today they'd call her hyperactive or ADD or something li</span>
  <span id="freeText1753129113652729177" style="display:none">I usually avoid "wonderful teacher" books like the plague, but then those books are often written by the teacher him/herself, or ghost-written with their approval, and can be oh so self-congratulatory. This one, however, was written by a student whose school experiences truly formed her character.<br /><br />Totto-chan is expelled from school (in first grade!) for being disruptive. She's not hostile or violent, just curious and easily bored. I suppose today they'd call her hyperactive or ADD or something like that. In 1939 Japan, she was just impossible. Most Western readers can have no idea how humiliating her expulsion would be, for her entire family. Her mother takes her to a tiny little experimental school where the head teacher loves children and wants them to be just that--children. Not little robots who obey rules and dress alike and chant slogans. We learn that many of Totto-chan's classmates are handicapped, deformed, "slow" or just "different"--like Miyazaki, the boy who was born in the US and doesn't know Japanese customs or have a good grasp of the language.<br /><br />The school itself is highly experimental--no regimentation, no set times for subjects, no chalk-and-talk (unless the chalk is used on the floor!). The classrooms themselves are train cars, the classes are tiny (just 50 students in all), and the teachers are flexible. Some of the teaching techniques would be revolutionary even today--imagine letting kids swim together naked!--but if a prurient fuss is not made about the naked body, they soon learn that boys are one way and girls another without any nonsense. <br /><br />The tales are told from a small child's perspective, which adds to its credibility. How else could she be so oblivious to the effects of war on Tokyo, until the bombers came? Because a five or six year old child is usually totally self-absorbed. What doesn't impinge directly on their world is unimportant, if it exists at all. Events may not have been just as presented, however they are what the child-Totto remembers. But not everything is lovely in the Tomoe garden...Totto-chan also has to deal with death and loss. The story of the disappearance of the family pet was heart-wrenching. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Her family sends her off to spend the summer with relatives, and when she returns her beloved dog is gone. Her mom tells her "Rocky disappeared", but I bet that in wartime Japan they simply could no longer afford to feed him. It reminded me of all my childhood friends whose dogs "ran away"--while they were at school, or summer camp, or whatever.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Tomoe school sounds like a lot of fun, and the book is an excellent read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1753129113652729177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13158599782090483272" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1477358146i/20941516._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I can see now why this childhood memoir is popular worldwide. It's a wonderful story advocating for respect, joy of learning and being kind to oneself and those around you.<br><br>The author Tetsuko Kuroyanagi was born in 1933 Japan. The story of this novel is set around the time when she was 5-6 years old, meaning just a few years before the Pacific War erupted. <br><br>Kuroyanagi wrote this book in dedication of her headmaster Mr. Kuboyashi. When Kuroyanagi first started her primary education, she was kicked out of school for being too restless, noisy and disturbing class. Her mother searched around for a new school and had a stroke of luck when she found a small school headed by the visionary Mr. Kuboyashi. Mr. Kuboyashi doesn't believe in the traditional teaching method of punishing children and forcing upon them a curriculum that is rigid and outdated. Instead he wants to foster well-being in every child by advocating independent learning according to your interest, learning about where food comes from during lunch break, and making each child realize that it is alright to be in tune with nature because there's something good in each one of them.<br><br>One thing I really enjoyed about this book was the narrator's acknowledgement about how much things have changed since then (especially toilets!). <br><br>This book was a nice break from all the depressing and pessimistic news I've been hearing about the world now. </span>

<div id="review_133231201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133231201" />
    <a title="Nathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3125102-nathan"><img alt="Nathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1269806247p2/3125102.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133231201?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan" href="/user/show/3125102-nathan">Nathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3125102-nathan?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3125102-nathan?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3125102-nathan?shelf=read-2010">read-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133231201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14215845649744878006">As a memoir, the book is unusual. It only spans a few years and during these years the narrator can be no older than about 9. It isn't exactly what you'd expect from Tetsuko Kuroyanagi either. Nothing is explicitly laid out as to how she decided to get into television, theater for the deaf, or how she became the first UNICEF ambassador. The focus was on much more basic aspects of the authors personality and where they came from. The stories are funny, sad, heartwarming, and universal. You don't </span>
  <span id="freeText14215845649744878006" style="display:none">As a memoir, the book is unusual. It only spans a few years and during these years the narrator can be no older than about 9. It isn't exactly what you'd expect from Tetsuko Kuroyanagi either. Nothing is explicitly laid out as to how she decided to get into television, theater for the deaf, or how she became the first UNICEF ambassador. The focus was on much more basic aspects of the authors personality and where they came from. The stories are funny, sad, heartwarming, and universal. You don't need to know the author to appreciate the book, nor do you have to be familiar with Japan and Japanese culture.<br /><br />However, the book is much more than a memoir. The primary merits of the book lie in its presentation, and endorsement, of a model of an alternate elementary education system. At first I couldn't help but think of the school as anything other than an early hippie "go with the flow, no stress, grow naturally" type of system. I wasn't wrong in that, but by the end I was convinced that, skillfully done, the system would actually be very good. <br /><br />I would highly recommend this book to anyone involved in education. I'd also throw it out there to anyone was interested in a fun, quick to read, and emotional biography.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14215845649744878006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86373259" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18426003088814708997">This is a charming memoir by a famous Japanese television personality about her time at the wonderfully unconventional school she attended in Tokyo in the early 1940s. At Tomoe School, there were no buildings as all the classrooms were in disused railroad cars, children were encouraged to study what they wanted when they wanted, in the warm weather they all swam nude in the pool without self consciousness, they learned respect for themselves and for each other, and apparently most of them grew u</span>
  <span id="freeText18426003088814708997" style="display:none">This is a charming memoir by a famous Japanese television personality about her time at the wonderfully unconventional school she attended in Tokyo in the early 1940s. At Tomoe School, there were no buildings as all the classrooms were in disused railroad cars, children were encouraged to study what they wanted when they wanted, in the warm weather they all swam nude in the pool without self consciousness, they learned respect for themselves and for each other, and apparently most of them grew up to be very happy self-reliant people. Although the book was delightful (and *how* I wish my own children could go to Tomoe), it had no narrative arc to speak of, being just a continuous series of small vignettes, and this made it very easy to put down and not pick up again. However, my interest picked up towards the end, as World War II cast its sad shadow, and the last third I read in one sitting. This is a special book, and was a huge best-seller in Japan when it was first published. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18426003088814708997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3957430465070010500" style="display:none">There is no way I am ging to able to write a conventional "review" of this book. It is a book that read before I knew what it meant for the books to be close to your heart. But, subconsciously, I have always felt as if, I was ordered to read that book that night by a supreme force. I read this, staying up late before a very important exam (good thing, my mom does not use Goodreads :D ) and could not put it down. I got emotional, I got happy for the protagonist, felt sad and shared her sadness on the occasions she had to suffer brief and prolonged separation of her parents. I have seen the way she perceives the world, the way she realizes that she is different from everyone around. How these differences make her parents worried and sad.</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3322429747?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rutvij" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rutvij" href="/user/show/26215243-rutvij">Rutvij</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3322429747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4742820838502578425">This book was recommended to me by a friend of mine. She claimed that of all the books that she had read, this book had the most influence on her. So, before starting the book, I had high expectations. I am glad to say that this book definitely lived up to the expectations. <br/><br/>The book is a description of an extraordinary school in Japan. We get to experience this school via the lens of a little girl who attends the school. This girl called Totto chan gets rejected from all conventional schools bec</span>
  <span id="freeText4742820838502578425" style="display:none">This book was recommended to me by a friend of mine. She claimed that of all the books that she had read, this book had the most influence on her. So, before starting the book, I had high expectations. I am glad to say that this book definitely lived up to the expectations. <br /><br />The book is a description of an extraordinary school in Japan. We get to experience this school via the lens of a little girl who attends the school. This girl called Totto chan gets rejected from all conventional schools because of being too talkative and mischievous. So, after getting disappointed one more time, the parents try sending their child to an unconventional school. But this unconventional school, to my surprise, is a model for how any school should teach their students. The story is essentially about the daily activities of Totto Chan in this school -- what she studies, who she becomes friends with, and (most importantly) how this prepares her to become a compassionate and kind person. <br /><br />This school run by the visionary Mr Kobayashi takes me back to the memories of a small evening school that I volunteered for at some point in my life. I wish I would have read this book earlier so that I could have taken some of the ideas from Mr Kobayashiâ€™s school. Of course, I do not agree with all the ideas of Mr Kobayashi, but nonetheless they are very well thought and well-executed by him. I would definitely recommend reading this book, especially to professionals involved (or who take an interest) in the education system throughout the world. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4742820838502578425" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('aWC8F6X+FAvPWJ4IQg10/izUdcYZwphjR56jOD3LWDcG40VbIKntmnu8PgvgdZBpFewDyL6rZElcmQ0L6Sh3oQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/188760.Tetsuko_Kuroyanagi">About Tetsuko Kuroyanagi</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/188760.Tetsuko_Kuroyanagi"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1342679352p3/188760.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/188760.Tetsuko_Kuroyanagi">Tetsuko Kuroyanagi
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1849343170537511645">Tetsuko Kuroyanagi is an internationally famous Japanese actress, a talk show host, a best-selling author of children book.<br/><br/>She founded the Totto Foundation, named for the eponymous and autobiographical protagonist of her book <i>Totto-chan, The Little Girl at the Window.</i> The Foundation professionally trains deaf actors, implementing Kuroyanagi's vision of bringing theater to the deaf.<br/><br/>In 1984, in reco</span>
  <span id="freeText1849343170537511645" style="display:none">Tetsuko Kuroyanagi is an internationally famous Japanese actress, a talk show host, a best-selling author of children book.<br /><br />She founded the Totto Foundation, named for the eponymous and autobiographical protagonist of her book <i>Totto-chan, The Little Girl at the Window.</i> The Foundation professionally trains deaf actors, implementing Kuroyanagi's vision of bringing theater to the deaf.<br /><br />In 1984, in recognition of her charitable works, Kuroyanagi was appointed to be a Goodwill Ambassador for UNICEF, being the first person from Asia to hold this position. During the late 1980s and the 1990s, she visited many developing countries in Asia and Africa for charitable works and goodwill missions, helping children who had suffered from disasters and war as well as raising international awareness of the situations of children in poor countries. Kuroyanagi has raised more than $20 million for the UNICEF programmes that she has been involved in, through television fund-raising campaigns. She also used the royalties from her bestselling book, Totto-chan, to contribute to UNICEF.<br /><br />In 1997, Kuroyanagi published the book <i>Totto-chan's Children</i>, which was based on her experience working for as a UNICEF Goodwill Ambassador from 1984 to 1996. Kuroyanagi is a director of the Japanese branch of the World Wildlife Fund.<br /><br />Kuroyanagi has twice brought America's National Theater of the Deaf to Japan, acting with them in sign language.<br /><br />For her involvement in media and television entertainment, Kuroyanagi won the Japanese Cultural Broadcasting Award, which is the highest television honour in Japan. Since then, she has been voted 14 times as Japanâ€™s favourite television personality, for the show Tetsukoâ€™s Room.[3]<br /><br />In 2000, Kuroyanagi became the first recipient of the Global Leadership for Children Award, which was established by UNICEF in the 10th anniversary of the 1990 World Summit for Children. In May 2003, Kuroyanagi received Order of the Sacred Treasure in recognition of her two decades of service for the worldâ€™s children.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1849343170537511645" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
